B006PTL1GC|4.0|David Cronenberg is most famous for his earlier work, which revolved around various incarnations of the New Flesh.  His three most recent films (shall we call it the Viggo Mortensen Trilogy?)  could be described as analyzing the New Identity.  Longtime fans have expressed disappointment, of course, although both A HISTORY OF VIOLENCE and EASTERN PROMISES are arguably the strongest overall films of Cronenberg's career.  A DANGEROUS METHOD is a nice end-cap to the prior two films, while never quite reaching their brutal eloquence.  This is perhaps because METHOD is more a movie of ideas, and ideas, while captivating, don't necessarily make for an entertaining film.  The story (such as it is) follows Carl Jung, as he rehabilitates Sabina Spielrein, discovering in her not only a brilliant young woman, but also a fierce lover.  This relationship causes friction between Jung and his mentor/friend, Sigmund Freud, and ultimately leads to the split between the two.  (No spoilers; it's history, after all.)<br /><br />This is a slow film; indeed, at times the audience's attention is tempted to waver (thus, the lack of a fifth star).  To look away would be to miss a fine little film, however.  The performances from the three leads is utterly captivating.  The film belongs to Michael Fassbender and Keira Knightley, the former restraining his baser urges, the latter exhibiting unrestrained emotional outbursts.  Viggo Mortensen is along in a supporting capacity, utterly disappearing into the role of a jovial, thoughtful Freud.  Turning in small, but excellent, supporting roles are Vincent Cassel (as Otto Gross) and Sarah Gadon as Emma Jung.  Christopher Hampton's script (based off of his play) is top-notch, and Cronenberg's direction is surprisingly controlled; for a director known for chaotic abandoned, Cronenberg plays the film fairly close to the chest, except for a few key scenes with Knightley.  Arguably, A DANGEROUS METHOD could have benefited from a little abandon; it's not boring, not at all, but the film is ultimately missing something.  Still, it's a fine addition to Cronenberg's cannon, one that leaves us hankering for his next disturbed offering.boring, not at all, but the film is ultimately missing something.  Still, it's a fine addition to Cronenberg's cannon, one that leaves us hankering for his next disturbed offering.

B0041B785Y|3.0|Koloss - 2012 - Meshuggah<br /><br />For me, the excitement and anticipation for a new Meshuggah album is unlike any other. Funny how my life has progressed over the years. I remember skipping class, heading to Last Unicorn to buy the newest album, taking an 8th of mushrooms, and listening to the album over and over again for hours and hours.  Years later I got out of work, went home to download the newest album on a desktop, smoked a couple grams of chronic and constantly rewound part after part of every song for hours and hours. Now the 2012 me pre-ordered Koloss, got it in the mail five days before the release date, waited three days until my next day off work, had a few glasses of wine and listened to the album once, start to finish, with no interruptions. My how times change.<br /><br />I have started listening to the album more and more to gather a good perspective of the end result. Right off the bat it must be said; Koloss is a terrific metal album. It must be considered one of the best, if not the best metal album of 2012. But its hard to see anything coming out in the next nine months that can top this. I do have to mention the new Cannibal Corpse album, great release by a great band, but no Koloss.<br /><br />As a Meshuggah album I just found it to be good, and any Meshuggah album thats just good is a major let down to any true fan. Meshuggah is known for pushing the envelope for every release they muster, and they most certainly did that with Koloss. I just cant stop thinking of this release as being one step forward and two steps back. Yes, they calmed down on being so \\"anal\\" about their approach for song writing and recording, but is that enough? Initially I was excited to hear this for myself, but it became a minor let down in a way. The let down was not so much the recording process, but the writing process. The most notable difference being the organic feel of the recording and mastering of the album, largely do to Meshuggah not mastering their album themselves. This has been the first time taking this approach since 1998. They brought back Daniel Bergstrand to do the job. He was used for the production of Destroy Erase Improve and Chaosphere. He did a great job making Koloss sound how Meshuggah wanted the songs they wrote to sound like; raw and organic, like passed Meshuggah records. But the writing...<br /><br />Since obZen we have all come to expect the most limitless and technical song structures only meshuggah can produce. Even while listening to Destroy Erase Improve you cant help but think of it being way before it's time. To this day when I listen to that album I think to myself \\" Why was I listening to Life Is Peachy then! \\" But with Koloss its almost like they wanted to make a statement to all the so called \\"djent\\" metal bands out there, saying \\"to all the bands who ripped us off, listen to this, this is how you do it, this is how you should sound\\". For those of you who don't know, djent is a metal genre that has already been played out in its very short existence. Meshuggah started the genre and crappy bands have ruined it. Most djent metal bands are not memorable, and that is the case of Koloss, a high majority of forgettable tracks. Honestly it's the most forgettable Meshuggah album to date.<br /><br />Koloss reminds me of a very less technical version of Nothing, and all Meshuggah fans know how technical Nothing is, technical, but not very.  While I listen to Koloss I can hear a lot of influences Meshuggah used from their previous albums; most notable being Nothing.<br /><br />Just to give an idea of my enjoyment of a few Meshuggah releases, I'm going to break it down in a percentage rating so you can see where I'm coming from: Destroy Erase Improve-85%, Chaosphere-90%, Nothing-75%, Catch ThirtyThree-100%, obZen-90%. But with Koloss I can say I only enjoyed ruffly 50% of it. Yes, the album sounds great and heavy, I do appreciate the organic raw vibe they were going with, but its the song structures that bother me the most.<br /><br />Do You remember when James Hetfield and Lars Ulrich asked Jason Newsted and Kirk Hammett for song ideas for Load? And we all know what happened to Metallica after that. I'm not saying in the instance of bands like Slipknot, Cradle of Filth, or In Flames, changing there whole writing process to appease the masses, but rather when a band uses ideas from other band members who should never have been listened to in the first place to come up with fresh ideas for their new albums. That happens far to much and you can actually sense a little bit of that going on with Koloss. The two worst songs on the album came from  Mårten Hagström and Jens Kidman. If that doesn't come to an end we're going to have a problem.<br /><br />This is the first Meshuggah album I've ever heard with great parts in the middle of decent songs. Usually all Meshuggah songs are enjoyable from beginning to end. Never would I have thought I'd have to sift through Meshuggah songs to find anything worthy, not worth it for any band.<br /><br />Track One: I Am Colossus: 2/5<br />  As soon as the track started, I was scratching my head wondering why this was the opener. It's a late middle track at best. Yes, the track lets you know the album will sound great, but made me skeptical of the song structures early. This track is a so so song with a couple good parts. A typical head banging song but not in the great Meshuggah way.<br /><br />Track Two: The Demons Name is Surveillance: 4/5<br />  As soon as the track started my hopes went threw the roof. I felt trapped on a runaway train going a thousand miles an hour. I was doomed! This song hits you fast and hard. It doesn't stop pummeling you in expected Meshuggah fashion. This song is in my top three favorite tracks.<br /><br />Track Three: Do Not Look Down: 3/5<br />  As soon as the track started my hopes came down a little bit. It's an interesting song but seems lazy to me, to boring even for Meshuggah. Typical choppyguitars with odd time signatures. Pretty decent song none the less.<br /><br />Track Four: Behind The Sun: 1/5<br />  Worst song on the album. Just simply not a good song. It gets a one instead of a zero because of the good ending, and also because it's ending.<br /><br />Track Five: The Hurt That Finds You First: 2/5<br />  Decent song I guess. To many tracks like this on the album already. I do enjoy the time signatures and Fredrick's work, but barely.<br /><br />Track Six: Marrow: 3/5<br />  Another good song with great parts. Although its a nice shift from where the album has been taking the listener. Finally.<br /><br />Track Seven: Break Those Bones Whose Sinews Gave It Motion: 4/5<br />  The thick and choppy Meshuggah that I love. Great intro, great verses and chorus. Great transitions, great bridge and ending. GREAT song. Beautiful Fredrick work. Nicely done Mårten. This is in my top three favorite tracks.<br /><br />Track Eight: Swarm: 4/5<br />  Smile on my face from start to finish. It has an old school Meshuggah feel. Great song. Almost on my top three tracks.<br /><br />Track Nine: Demiurge: 4/5<br />  A song completely written by Mårten Hagström and unlike the other tracks he co wrote with other members that I do not like, I actually love this song. It's like a song not written by Meshuggah but sounds like Meshuggah. Refreshing. In my top three favorite tracks.<br /><br />Track Ten: The Last Vigil: 5/5<br />  I love when Meshuggah does this. Whether it be a part of a song or a whole song, I'm just a sucker for it. Its a beautiful and haunting journey. The track is like \\"Acrid Placidity\\" off DEI and \\"Sum\\" off Catch ThirtyThree. Just great. The band was debating on putting this track in the middle of the album.  The flow of the album would have been completely diminished if it came during track four or five, but it's perfect at the end. It's a soothing breather after such a brutal album.<br /><br />Fredrick Thordendal: 3/5<br />  Great sound like always. A lot less signature frederick on this album though, less solos and what not. Brains behind the song structures, just not at the top of his game with this album.<br /><br />Mårten Hagström: 3/5<br />  Great sound like always. He did write some ruff material on this album, but wrote one of my favorite songs also. Fredrick and Mårten are a great guitar duo.<br /><br />Thomas Haake: 4/5<br />  The reason Meshuggah is so damn good and has set the bar so high in not only metal music but all music is the drummer. Thomas Haake is a god send. Although he lightened up on his amazing technical ability on this one, he still shows all why he is the greatest metal drummer on the planet.<br /><br />Jens Kidman: 5/5<br />  Always sounds amazing. Always perfect syllable placement for the words. His screams are less growling on this album, and more clear. I like the change up. It's like Chaosphere and obZen molded together.  He is what a metal singer should sound like.<br /><br />Dick Lövgren: -<br />  Has no writing input what so ever. Plays the bass how Meshuggah wants him to, then Meshuggah makes it sound how they want it to sound.<br /><br />Lyrics: 4/5<br />  Thomas Haake is the greatest lyricist in music. He shows this on every Meshuggah album, especially on Koloss. The worst song on Koloss has the worst lyrics, Haakes can't nail it every time.<br /><br />Artwork/Title: 2/5<br />  When I saw the artwork and heard the title for the album, I was skeptical to say the least. When I saw the obZen cover and read the name I was floored. They usually take great direction with the visuals. But after hearing the album Im just satisfied with the artwork/title. It fits the album. Didn't like how they reprised the snakes from Catch ThirtyThree though.<br /><br />Although Koloss was some what of a let down, you can't help but enjoy how brutally heavy it is and how great the sound turned out. It's the most logical step Meshuggah could have taken toward their discography.

0439023513|3.0|I'm a bit late on \\"The Hunger Games\\" boat, as I just finished reading the final book last night.<br /><br />I don't hate the ending. I don't necessarily even dislike it. However, I do think it's the weakest book out of the trilogy.<br /><br />Spoilers ahead.<br /><br />.<br />.<br />.<br />.<br />.<br />.<br />.<br />.<br />.<br />.<br /><br />I'm not surprised by whom Katniss picks, and why, or about the fact that President Coin dies, but I do find the fact that Coin dies at the hands of Katniss a bit unbelievable. Don't get me wrong. It seems pretty obvious from the beginning that Coin is both shrewd and dangerous, and that she has it out for Katniss. But Katniss herself has been either too naive, too preoccupied with her own pain, or too unable to relate to other people to truly get it. In fact, Haymitch and other people have to explain Coin (and many others) to her. Katniss has such a hard time making up her mind between Peeta and Gale, or about other people's motivations, yet she suddenly sees both Coin and Snow so clearly in the middle of her grief over her sister, the one person she cares about above all others in the world. She suddenly gets that while Snow does think people are expendable, he doesn't like to \\"waste\\" them... Really? Hunter instincts and understanding her prey aren't good explanations, as Katniss kept going into hysterics anytime she felt Snow's presence in the roses, etc., and had to be sedated several times. I don't see how she could be level-headed enough to see the truth behind Snow's words, especially considering her longstanding and supposedly all-consuming and unquenchable vendetta against him. It's true that Katniss is decisive when she's angry, but she mainly reacts to loss, and takes out whomever is responsible for taking that person away from her. In this case, Coin has chosen to remain relatively distant, and there's no real evidence to connect her to Prim's death so soon after the fact, only Snow's word and Katniss' feeling that Coin doesn't especially like her. It's also true that Snow would have been executed either way, but Katniss was so consumed by the need to do it herself that she made it a condition of her cooperation with Coin. She did not want justice, she wanted revenge, so it makes no sense to me to not do it herself. I suppose I understand why, in a book where there is a protagonist and so much symbolism and so much responsibility attached to her, Katniss would have to be the one to kill President Coin, but in real life, I think she would have been too much in her own fog to do it. I think other people would have had to step up to the plate to take out Coin, and in fact, I did wonder why no one else was doing so even before this time.<br /><br />I'm upset that Prim was killed off, simply because I liked her. However, I get that this is war, and I'm not upset enough to think that the rules of engagement should have been bent for her / the readers, or to think that war ever spares the good people or is moral or fair. I would hate to ever be in their situation and stoop down to other people's level, the way Gale does, but I recognize that, sometimes, while it may not be absolutely necessary in order to win the war, it may be the most expedient way, and possibly necessary to save further lives, both physically and emotionally. War makes for desperate people, and in the midst of it, the unconscionable may seem like the only response possible.<br /><br />The rest of the ending is positively cheerful, though, in comparison to the rest of the books. When Katniss finally has kids, I can hear someone off-screen saying, \\"Ah, the persistence of life... It is wonderful.\\" It's a bit heavy-handed and odd. Most people have been damaged by war, but Katniss especially. She was actually a tribute in the Hunger Games. She knew her fellow district tribute going into the first game, and she grew to love him, even if she didn't know it herself for way too long (seriously, I wanted to smack her when she started going back and forth to Gale again). She became a symbol, used and abused by both sides to further their own agendas. She lost practically everyone she cared about, not simply due to the hunger and disease which accompany poverty, or even through death in combat, but through the same manipulations that tore her down. I understand that since the old government has been overthrown and Coin has been removed, the Hunger Games are no longer an issue. But the damage was emotional, and it ran deep, and the simple cessation of the games shouldn't be enough, even after 10 years, to convince her that it's safe. If anything, Coin's suggestion to hold one last symbolic Hunger Games proves that it wasn't the Capitol, per se, which was corrupt, but that people all have the capacity to be evil. People are what's not safe, so how can you possibly feel safe just because one government has fallen, and another has risen in its place? (Note: I don't personally believe this; I just don't see how someone like Katniss would not see it in a sinister light.) This is especially true since Katniss' support system seems to consist mainly of Peeta, who is himself incredibly damaged; Prim is gone, Gale recognizes that Katniss will never be able to see him without thinking of Prim's death and so goes away, Katniss' mother is so wracked by loss and grief that she cannot return to district 12 at all, Greasy Sae wasn't present at the most horrific experiences, Haymitch isn't well-equipped to deal with emotional baggage himself, and Annie didn't have an especially strong relationship with Katniss throughout the book. I don't find Katniss' transformation, or Peeta getting well so relatively quickly in the book, and then enough to make Katniss well, too, believable. It was a very fast and too-tidy wrap-up, all things considered. I mean, she's even doing \\"therapy\\" with writing down her story, even though for the first 20 years of her life, she was wary of people and connecting with them, or of introspection. I think it would have been more realistic if she had gone the way of Haymitch, drowning out her sorrows (not necessarily through alcohol, though), or at least pretending they don't exist or bother her, rather than revisiting them voluntarily.he started going back and forth to Gale again). B006PTL1QM|5.0|A DANGEROUS METHOD offers the audience a look at the beginnings of psychoanalysis by presenting the lives of four important doctors at eh turn of the 20th century. The film is adapted by Christopher Hampton from his play `The Talking Cure' which in turn was a dramatization of John Kerr's book `A Most Dangerous Method'. An interesting aspect of the film is that it is primarily a film about discussions embroidered with enough action to make it cinematic. It is beautiful to watch, a pleasure to hear (Howard Shore's musical score consists of `arrangements' of themes form the operas of Richard Wagner with an emphasis on the `Siegfried Idyll' which as a work with its own similar history that plays exceptionally well into this story), a challenge to absorb in the hands of director David Cronenberg, and a showcase for some fine acting.<br /><br />The film opens in 1904 with Carl Jung (Michael Fassbender) accepting as a patient young Russian &eacute;migr&eacute; Sabina Spielberg (Kiera Knightley) who is a tortured soul from a past abusive upbringing.  We are like a fly on the wall, observing Sabina's neurotic/psychotic behavior as Jung begins to tame her: their gradually successful treatment results in an affair both emotional and physical despite the fact that Jung is married to Emma (Sarah Gadon) and has children and also despite the fact that Jung believes the doctor/patient relationship should be respected. Meanwhile off in Vienna Sigmund Freud (Viggo Mortensen) is gaining acclaim for this psychoanalytic work and refers a patient of his, a fellow analyst Dr. Otto Gross (Vinvent Cassell) whose extreme sexual acting out has gained notoriety. Jung accepts Gross as a patient and from the stance of the story, Jung learns more form Gross than Gross benefits from Jung: apparently Jung's libido is encouraged and that urges him to act out with Sabina, now able to respond to the world in a manner so appropriate that she becomes a medical student and eventually a psychiatrist also.  The story also examines the coming together and the eventual parting of ways between Jung and Freud and it is this part of the film that becomes more significantly and satisfyingly cerebral.  It is this intense relationship between Freud and Jung that gives us a quality understanding of the birth of psychoanalysis.<br /><br />Though the opening scenes of the film - those that focus on the outrageous behavioral changes in Sabina - becoming trying on the nerves, the remainder of the film is intellectually satisfying. Kiera Knightley stumbles with her Russian accent and tends to take her character's mental aberrations too far, but otherwise she is excellent in her role.  Both Michael Fassbender and Viggo Mortensen offer elegant and understated characterizations of these two important men in the history of medicine.  Vincent Cassell and Sarah Gadon are also exceptional in their acting. Providing more in depth studies of both Freud and Jung would have been welcome, but in the confines of a film we at least are allowed insights into the minds of these two men. Grady Harp, March 12also examines the coming together and the eventual parting of ways between Jung and Freud and it is this part of the film that becomes more significantly and satisfyingly cerebral.  It is this intense relationship between Freud and Jung that gives us a quality understanding of the birth of psychoanalysis.<br /><br />Though the opening scenes of the film - those that focus on the outrageous behavioral changes in Sabina - becoming trying on the nerves, the remainder of the film is intellectually satisfying. 0307719219|3.0|I was not overly impressed by this book. The authors' writing style needs work, and their main argument is too simplistic: nations fail because of bad political and economic institutions, which are due to history.<br /><br />On a basic level, their thesis is right: a society that serves a narrow elite's interests will never achieve the same prosperity that a broad-based, inclusive society and economic system will. Politics matters for economic outcomes. The United States and Great Britain are cited as success stories, while many Latin American countries are apparently abject failures.<br /><br />This, the authors claim, is largely because of their institutional histories. Peru and Bolivia, for instance, suffered from the mining mita, a forced labour system Spanish conquistadors used to exploit the indigenous population. Meanwhile, the American colony of Jamestown experimented with and prospered under democracy and property rights. These early histories created persistence up until today.<br /><br />Yet Acemoglu and Robinson mask the fact that the Jamestown colony was also exploitative, albeit in a different way. The colony was founded within Powhatan Confederacy borders, without the indigenous people's permission - property rights were certainly violated. Skirmishes often broke out with the natives, and further North American colonization would rely on this \\"killing the Indians\\" model.<br /><br />Exploitation and oppression are necessary for nations to succeed. The authors, however, do not categorize what type of oppression is required. Instead, they simply consider all oppression bad, ignoring the cruel subtleties of history. The mita was terrible, but the genocide of North American natives to clear the land for white settlers was no better. Institutions matter, yet many successful ones are plagued by an evil past.<br /><br />The intricacies of international relations are also neglected by Acemoglu and Robinson. The reason Latin America is so poor today is not dueto colonial history, but recent U.S. foreign policy. It is no secret that the United States supported dictators like Batista (Cuba), Banzer (Bolivia), and Armas (Guatemala), so that American corporations could prosper in Latin America. A similar argument applies to Africa and the Middle East.<br /><br />If we take Acemoglu and Robinson's argument to its logical extreme, then we can think of the world as a failed nation. The elite (OECD countries) create extractive institutions that exploit the poor in Africa, Latin America, The Middle East and Asia. Indeed the United States still supports dictatorships in Saudi Arabia and Jordan, and until recently supported Hosni Mubarak's reign in Egypt.<br /><br />If Acemoglu and Robinson had a better grasp of history and international politics, then perhaps Why Nations Fail would be a more informative read.

B000QCS8TW|5.0|I place the blame on hobbits and boy wizards, but I have never been a fan of any book that classifies itself as \\"fantasy.\\" Oh sure, I can and do enjoy urban fantasies featuring vampires, werewolves and the like ... but toss in hobbits with the details of all their many meals during the day and boy wizards who you know in advance will be standing by the time the book or series comes to an end, and I run the opposite way with all possible haste.<br /><br />That was before I was introduced to George R.R. Martin and the world of Westeros.<br /><br />Frankly, I had never heard of Martin nor his books before a friend encouraged me to watch the HBO production of A Game of Thrones. I'm still trying to figure out why the series clicked with me, but it did. So, too, did the first book in the series. I managed to read through the book in a week, something that I can rarely do with an 800-plus page book. By the time I finished, my preconceptions about fantasy novels had been shattered. I've even jumped straight into the second book of the series, so anixous to continue the plot threads of so many richly drawn characters laid out in the opening book of the series.<br /><br />To be sure, there are some fantasy elements to the book such as references to dragons, white walkers (who seem to be a bit like vampires) and the Little People of the Forest (perhaps the faerie folk?). But the emphasis is on the human characters. These aren't the type of characters you can group into Good and Evil camps. The best and most noble of characters can do bad things and the worst characters can surprise you by doing the right things sometimes. These are fully realized human characters in all their glorious shades of gray.<br /><br />It often seems that the \\"magic\\" overshadows the human elements in fantasies, but in A Game of Thrones it seems this \\"rule\\" has been turned on its head. There are no hair-breadth escapes with the aid of a magic wand or an incantation. Swords don't glow with magical intent. The people of the north may still go to sacred groves to talk to their gods, but if the gods choose to answer it is not with any sort of human voice. In other words, the tale holds together on a rational level without resorting to supernatural intervention.<br /><br />Even though some plot points may require a leap of faith, I found myself willing to make that leap with Martin and his characters. Oh sure, there are elements I still scratch my head over, like how winter can be brief or last for a decade or how the humans can trace their history back thousands of years but seem to progress so slowly when it comes to technology and innovation. Perhaps these questions will be answered later in the series, but for now I'm intent to experience Westeros as Martin intended it.<br /><br />Perhaps there will come a time as I read further into the series when I come to a point and say \\"Oh, that's preposterous!\\" and put the series down never to return. But for now I've gone farther than I ever imagined I would with the series and hope to go even further. What I do know is that if all \\"fantasies\\" were this detailed and grounded in a more real world, I would have been a fan long ago.t. What I do know is that if all \\"fantasies\\" were this detailed and grounded in a more real world, I would have been a fan long ago.

B0002KQNZO|3.0|It's easy to be a completist where Art Blakey's music is concerned.  The percussionist was such a force of nature that \\"Blakey reunion bands\\" simply can not approach the power, energy and drive that Bu, only, was capable of.  But of the 50+ recordings that I've collected by him, the ones from the '60s and '80s receive a lot less attention than the music from the '50s and the '70's.<br /><br />Little American music can compare with the Blakey '50s albums.  The genius of Clifford Brown sparkles thoughout the two sets recorded in Birdland, and \\"Art Blakey and the Jazz Messenger\\" (on Columbia, not Blue Note) exhibits Horace Silver at his very peak as a composer (\\"Nica's Dream\\") and arranger (\\"Ecaroh\\").  Additionally, the horns of Hank Mobley and Donald Byrd are incessantly tasteful, complementary, and melodic--every inspired note has a purpose, every solo is structured to yield a musical result.<br /><br />The '70s were tough times for Art, but he produced some amazing music, thanks to the advanced, challenging compositions of Walter Davis, Jr. and the mixing  of new blood (tenor saxophonist Dave Schntter, who would have the longest tenure as a tenor player with Art) and the deceptively pyrotechnical Bill Hardman (a trumpeter who, in addition to being a Messenger in three different decades, was game for a cutting contest with Freddie, or whomever, whenever the occasion arose).<br /><br />I'm aware that this particular album has been singled out by some major, recognizable names in jazz as a personal favorite.  But at the same time it's easy to forget that busy woodshedding musicians don't necessarily have the time to collect and listen to as much music as, say, a Blakey \\"completist.\\"  The '60s' bands with Shorter, Hubbard (sometimes Lee Morgan), and Fuller were capable of much lightning and thunder, but after a while they pale alongside the musicianship of the earlier '50s recordings and the adventurous overachievers of the '70s.  There's plenty of thunder and lightning but, upon close, repeated listening, there's more smoke than fire on the '60s' sextet recordings, of which \\"Free for All\\" is a prime example.<br /><br />Listen, for example, to the title song and each soloist's attempt to \\"stir it up\\" on the tune's single tone center for the duration of the solo.  Shorter is reduced to meaningless and repeated scale patterns and even curious trills as though he were looking at the seconds remaining before he can give way to Hubbard.  Freddie puts on his usual spectacular demonstration, again more intent on merely creating \\"excitement,\\" or manufactured sound and intensity rather than meaningful music.  Fuller, as is quite often the case on his sessions with Blakey, is the weak link in the frontline of solos.<br /><br />Blakey collectors--like collectors of Ellington's vast repertory--are bound to disagree about which is the best, or most representative, Blakey album.  I would in a heartbeat give away \\"Free for All\\" before letting go of a number of \\"live\\" 2-disc volumes--[[ASIN:B00005MIZ8 A Night at Birdland, Vol. 1]] with Clifford motivated to \\"let it all out\\" along with the announcements of Pee Wee Marquette and Art himself (dig his explanation of his role as an onlooker to Dizzy's composing of \\"Night in Tunisia\\"): [[ASIN:B002HJ5X2C At Jazz Corner of World Vo]] At the Jazz Corner of the World\\" (Morgan and Mobley, a match made in heaven; [[ASIN:B00005UMTX Meet You at the Jazz Corner of the World]] (Morgan and Shorter--Wayne is the creative thinker, working with the musical idea, or motif; Mobley is always the \\"singer,\\" playing from the heart while favoring long and lyrical lines, or melodies, over creative \\"ideas,\\" or motifs); last, [[ASIN:B00074C660 At Cafe Bohemia]] Dorham and Mobley, as with Lee and Donald Byrd, Hank's musical partnership with K.D. is telepathic).  Each of the foregoing places the listener in in front of the bandstand, with Art himself (esp. at the Cafe Bohemia) inviting the audience to take off their shoes, kickup their feet, and swing in their seats for an entire night's worth of exciting, inventive, passionate, often explosive, music.<br /><br />I agree that \\"Free for All\\" belongs in the collection of any Blakey follower, representing Art's \\"hard boppish\\" version of the modal music that proved so successful for Miles on the immortal \\"Kind of Blue\\" session.  Moreover, for those of us who prefer more chords and melody, \\"Free for All\\" has a welcome change of pace in the form of \\"Pensativa,\\" the jazz standard by the late Clare Fischer. (In that respect, \\"Free for All\\" resembles Miles' programming, for \\"Kind of Blue,\\" of \\"Freddie Freeloader,\\" a finger-snapping blues, inserted for the sake of variety in the otherwise concentrated playing of the modal tracks.  But overall the album did not live up to its advance billing for at least this listener (Later qualifier: mine is quite obviously a minority report.  As one commenter said, \\"It was my entrance into Blakey's music.\\"  Frankly, I think my own son might have the same response.  He simply didn't come of age when Louis, Bing and Sinata, or Billie, Ella and Sarah were singing all of those \\"standards\\" from the Great American Songbook.  After 1960 Bob Dylan's and the Beatles's music understandably \\"muted\\" the music of, for example, Cole Porter and George Gershwin. And Mobley's lyrical, melodic imagination requires a listener familiar with the standards of the past to a greater degree than the adventurous, \\"idea-motivated\\" solos of a Shorter, Henderson or late Coltrane.<br /><br /> Is one approach better, or more valid, than the other? Of course not.  But if Tony Bennett and Lady Gaga can make music together (which they do, and with credibility), then it's certainly possible for listeners and musicians to get a foot planted in both worlds.  Coltrane started in the Songbook, and when he ventured away from it, his new modal approach was directly influenced by the great Broadway composer, Richard Rodgers (in fact, he continued to play Rodgers' \\"My Favorite Things\\" right up to the end of his life).  Moreover, as has been pointed out by numerous scholars and musicians,  Coltrane's revolutionary reinvention of harmonies on the seminal \\"Giant Steps\\" album follows the unusual, almost head-spinning, harmonic pattern of the bridge Richard Rodgers wrote for a tune entitled: \\"Have You Met Miss Jones?\\"<br /><br />I'll leave it there, except for calling attention to an indispensable Blakey album that tends to be overlooked simply because it was recorded on Columbia rather than Blue Note. This isn't the same as the more popular album with the same title--\\"Art Blakey and the Jazz Messengers\\" (the so-called \\"Moanin'\\" date). This Columbia album spotlights Horace Silver's brilliant compositions and arrangements (\\"Nica's Dream,\\" \\"Ecarole\\") along with Hank Mobley, Donald Byrd, and Doug Watkins.  The album features beautifully balanced sound along with equal focus on the individual soloists' and the composer's creativity  [[ASIN:B0012GMYFK The Jazz Messengers]].<br /><br />And finally the most overlooked Messengers--the \\"overachievers\\" of the fusion-heavy 1970s. Their recordings, all made offshore, are scarce and often hard to find, but this one DVD, recorded with the Messengers performing in the streets and under a high sun in an Italian village for the Umbria Jazz Festival, does justice by both Art's mainstream emissaries and the visionary compositions of his chief composer-arranger, Walter Davis, Jr.: [[ASIN:B00008G8H3 Art Blakey's Jazz Messengers]]for an entire night's worth of exciting, inventive, passionate, often explosive, music. Kaladin has character, sure, but it's just not deep enough for me to accept it as the best Sanderson could make for his main character. It's more like the invincible idol that is supposed to die to be revered by the real main character, who is either too shy to make an appearance in the first book, or doesn't exist. He's like the main character of a video game, as opposed to a book lead.<br />* I really hope Elhokar's character is expanded in later books, as other than Sanderson's core characters, (Jasnah, Shallan, Dalinar, Adolin, Wit, Kaladin, Renarin, Navani, Teft and Rock) No one could stand as their own person, and Elhokar is too important to be a cut-out on a stand, because if he falls over, it's gonna end badly.<br /><br />Summation: It's Sanderson's epic. His finest points are exemplified, and his shortfalls will be remedied in the second book, and if they're not, the plot is good enough not to see the faded sections in the sheet the first time you read it. I stress this, these points will only come up if you write, like me, and you read it a few times.<br />Grade: 4.5 out of 5. This book is excellent, unless you really like character-driven lit-fic. If you do, then this is not the fantasy book for you to begin with. That would be [[ASIN:0756405890 The Name of the Wind (Kingkiller Chronicles, Day 1)]]. However, if you like sci-fi for the plot and world, then Brandon's work will blow you away like a highstorm on a plain.

B003H4I4JU|5.0|I do not understand why, with the popularity of the first book, this one has so few reviews.  In my opinion, this book is much stronger.  While the first book does devote time towards dealing with themes that have managed to remain relevant long after its initial publication, this book goes into more detail exploring much more compelling questions.<br /><br />In \\"Ender's Game,\\" most of the book is devoted to Ender's time in the Battle School.  What we read about there is Ender's transition from little boy on Earth, a Third who experiences a difficult life on a planet experiencing societal difficulties, going off to train in the business of war.  While it is an arduous physical journey for Ender, and he does have some difficulty with psychological issues such as separation from family, learning how to compromise his ethics in order to survive, and learning how to lead other people, those psychological issues seemed secondary for much of the book.  Only at the end of the first story are we introduced with the psychological significance of what Ender was compelled to do.<br /><br />That is why I do not understand the apparent lack of people making the transition over to this, the follow-up.  All of the big questions seemed to hang out there, dangling like a teaser at the end.  How could anyone not continue on to see how Ender would cope with his xenocide, especially since it is revealed at the end of that book that a hive queen survived and was communicating with Ender?  How could they not continue on to see how his colony had turned out, how humanity had turned out after moving forward from the society Peter had left behind?<br /><br />All of these important questions are explored in \\"Speaker for the Dead,\\" and they are handled really well.  The concept of a Speaker for the Dead is fascinating, especially in the context of Ender's story.  Here is a boy who was essentially tricked into killing off an entire intelligent species, just to find that he had an opportunity for redemption.  First Ender writes the Hive Queen and the Hegemon, turning his own name into an epithet.  The humanity that once loved him as their savior has turned their back on him, casting him off as the personification of evil.  Later this redemption continues as Ender takes on the role of Speaker, teaching people that, as is explained in the book, good and evil both exist in the same heart.  He essentially primes people for understanding what he did, his actions and his motivations, hoping that one day he would earn his redemption.  \\"Here is the good, here is the bad.  No judgement.  Just the truth.\\"<br /><br />At first the fast forward three thousand years into the future seems ridiculous, but as the book continues it begins to make more sense.  We get to see a society that seems to have taken Ender's lessons in the Hive Queen and the Hegemon to heart, but as we learn in the book humanity only seems to have learned that lesson because it was convenient.  The true test comes when they encounter another sentient species.  Ender has changed, but has humanity really changed, even after three thousand years?<br /><br />In this book humanity gets another chance, this time with the benefit of hindsight.  Will they repeat their mistakes, or learn from them and handle things better this time around?  That is one of the central questions that is answered in this book.  The answer is made clear by the end of this book, so I will not spoil it in my review.<br /><br />The concept of the Hierarchy of Foreignness is also fascinating.  Anyone who has spent any time considering interaction with other species will be compelled by this story.  I know people of my generation have seen Cosmos, and likely remember the scene where Carl Sagan discusses the problems we would face when interacting with alien intelligent life.  To paraphrase:  \\"We can't even interact with the other intelligent life on our own planet.\\"  And then closer to today we have Stephen Hawking's speculation on how alien races would treat us if they encountered us first.  Anyone who has seen these things and engaged in any thought experiment will be compelled by this story as they read about individuals and a society struggle with determining where the piggies and buggers fall on this hierarchy.<br /><br />Maybe because we live in a time with no space operas on television, young people are not drawn to these ethical questions as much as those of us who grew up on shows like Star Trek.  We watched stories about humanity struggling against its worst impulses to be the people that they wanted to be.  So reading such a story seems pretty natural, and darn entertaining.  That is why I find \\"Speaker for the Dead\\" so much more compelling than \\"Ender's Game.\\"  It is also why I find the reviews bemoaning the lack of \\"action\\" of the first book.  Ender does not need to cave a Bonzo's nose into his brain in every book in order for significant, compelling action to have taken place.  \\"Speaker for the Dead\\" has actions in spades, as complex characters evolve and change in response to the events that they encounter.  You should not be disappointed by this book.ow alien races would treat us if they encountered us first. 1400052181|4.0|Rebecca Skloot's `The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks' is a riveting story of the birth of bioethics and highlights the legal battles that surrounds medical discovery to this day. The story is told from the perspective of a poor, black tobacco farmer whose cancer cells were taken without her knowledge, and the cells went on to become one of the most significant tools in modern medicine.    It is the 1950s, and we meet Henrietta Lacks, a poor, black tobacco farmer. In the prime of her life, at the age of 30, she is struck down by cervical cancer with tragic consequences for her young family. Unbeknownst to them, and without her consent, her doctor takes a piece of her of tumour and these cancer cells go on become the first tissue cells to grow and survive in cultures.    These immortal cells proved pivotal in the development of the polio vaccine. The HeLa cells, as they were called to protect Henrietta's identity, have since resulted in many scientific milestones, including cloning, gene mapping and in vitro fertilisation. They have even been sent up into space to see what would happen to cells in zero gravity. Needless to say, the HeLa cells have contributed to a multibillion-dollar medical industry.    For nearly a quarter of a century, Henrietta's family was completely unaware of her legacy and received no compensation. It was only 25 years after her death that scientists discovered that Henrietta's cells had contaminated hundreds of other tissue cultures. It turned out that HeLa cells could float on dust particles in the air and travel on unwashed hands, thereby contaminating other cultures.    A group of scientists decided to track down her relatives to take samples from them in the hope that they could use the family's DNA to map Henrietta's genes so they could distinguish Henrietta's cell cultures from others and sort out the controversial contamination problem. Henrietta's impoverished family became entangled in medical research they neither comprehended norbenefitted from, and once their mother's identity was revealed, their anger and frustration was understandable and expected.    Skloot, an award-winning writer who specialises in, what she calls, narrative science writing, first learned about Henrietta when she was a 16-year-old biology student. Skloot says she became quite obsessed with telling Henrietta's story, which was easier said than done. Henrietta's family resisted her overtures for over a year, finally collaborating with her once they were convinced she had their mother's story and legacy at heart.    Skloot's writing is gripping, but all the more so because this is no work of fiction - it's the true story of the groundbreaking HeLa line of cells that live on today in laboratories worldwide. Skloot's message is clear: Human beings drive scientific advances and, even with the best of intentions, things can and do go wrong. However, people are behind all human tissue samples and, medical and scientific advancement notwithstanding, they should have some say in what happens to their cells.    [This review was first published in CEO magazine. See [...] for more.]

0451169514|4.0|I recently got around to Stephen King's mammoth 1987 horror thriller, \\"It\\", which inexplicably this longtime King fan had never cracked open until now.  Well, I shouldn't say \\"inexplicably\\", because I'm sure it had something to do with that adjective \\"mammoth\\" combined with an adjective I often use to describe myself: \\"lazy\\".  Anyway, I finally got to the book and I liked it, often quite a bit.<br /><br />I didn't love it, however, finding it to be the first King story I've read that didn't fully justify its generous length.  It was also peppered with a few elements that sort of annoyed and/or frustrated me, again a new thing for me with King.<br /><br />But first let me be a gentleman and list a few of the many things I liked about this tale of seven kids who take on an elemental evil hiding out in the sewers of a Maine town in 1958 and then come together as adults in the 1980's to take it on again.  I liked the characterizations of the kids, who were sympathetic and interestingly complex, and the way King makes it clear that the ways children are raised inarguably have impacts on their personalities that will follow them for the rest of their lives.  I liked the individuals scenes of horror, which are effective, scary, and imaginative.  I enjoyed comparing the personalities of the kids with the personalities of their grown-up counterparts.<br /><br />I also cheered during the many scenes depicting heartless, cruel people in power finally getting their due from the people they regularly victimized and abused. Scenes involving an abusive husband in the modern-day scenes and a horrible bully in the 1950's part of the story immediately come to mind, and there are a couple of other satisying confrontations along those lines, too.  And these have little or nothing to do with the horror story at hand; they're just an added bonus.<br /><br />Now, what aspects of the book didn't wow me as much?  The story is constructed so that we continually jump back and forth between the kids battling the creature known as \\"It\\" in the 1950's and their re-match with \\"It\\" in the 1980's.  Because both stories happen simultaneously throughout the entire book and we cut back and forth between them, momentum and drama never really build up in each individual timeline, at least as much as they otherwise could.  Also, King comes up with (I thought) contrived reasons for the 1980's versions of the characters to not talk to one another about, or even think about, their original 1950's battle, so as not to spoil things for the reader (for one thing, think \\"mysterious memory loss\\").<br /><br />I also thought the exact nature of what was going on in both battles was ultimately murky, having something to do with a battle of wills on a higher plane of existence, but nothing I could better get my hands around.  The weird, godlike turtle creature sitting on the sidelines didn't exactly help matters in that regard.<br /><br />Finally, all of this- the first battle, the second battle, the individual lives of the characters as kids and as adults- was frequently interrupted by long history lessons about the town of Derry, Maine, the setting of both epic battles (the earthbound parts of them, anyway).  The Derry history sections are actually kind of interesting, often detailing horrendous occurrences that the \\"It\\" entity instigated, emotionally fed on, or both.  But in a story that was already of epic length, I wonder if the information in those sections couldn't have been communicated via briefer mechanisms.<br /><br />In the end, Stephen King is too talented a writer to not realize exactly what he was doing when he delivered an 1,100-plus page book about a bunch of kids taking on a creepy clown (the monster's preferred form of corporeal existence).  Perhaps after being burned by his publishers years before, who made him cut tens of thousands of words from his original draft of \\"The Stand\\", Mr. King decided to use the clout he eventually earned to write another epic story and make it as long as he saw fit, emphasizing- without interference this time- the immersive experience over pacing and conciseness.<br /><br />If the results are a little self-indulgent and well... long, so what?  It's not like the author writes this way all the time.  In my view, 95% of the time Stephen King's books are exactly as long as they need to be, so what's the harm if an otherwise fairly solid King novel, just this once, gives us a couple of hundred pages more carnage, characterization, and details than we actually need?  Or, in his zeal to be truly \\"out there\\" in some of the cosmic confrontations, we mutter a \\"huh?\\" from time to time?  Small sins, gentle reader, small sins.<br /><br />In the end, for me at least, I guess that creepy clown with the sharp teeth pulled this sometimes flawed, sometimes lumbering epic over the finish line.tory and make it as long as he saw fit, emphasizing- without interference this time- the immersive experience over pacing and conciseness.<br /><br />If the results are a little self-indulgent and well... long, so what?  It's not like the author writes this way all the time.  In my view, 95% of the time Stephen King's books are exactly as long as they need to be, so what's the harm if an otherwise fairly solid King novel, just this once, gives us a couple of hundred pages more carnage, characterization, and details than we actually need?  Or, in his zeal to be truly \\"out there\\" in some of the cosmic confrontations, we mutter a \\"huh?\\" from time to time?  Small sins, gentle reader, small sins.<br /><br />In the end, for me at least, I guess that creepy clown with the sharp teeth pulled this sometimes flawed, sometimes lumbering epic over the finish line.

But the author isn't exactly what Hazel expected, and neither is the end result of the trip.<br /><br />There's a lot to unpack in a John Green novel. On each of my trips, I flew into Gatwick airport, avoiding busy Heathrow, picked up a rental car (right-hand drive, manual transmission and motoring on the \\"wrong\\" side of the road--what fun!) and immediately set out for the countryside. My destinations included aviation, automobile and military museums, breweries, Stonehenge (of course) and \\"off the beaten path\\" attractions throughout southern England and, via the Channel Tunnel on one trip, in northern France. I gave London a wide berth simply because I generally prefer to avoid congested, chaotic, hard-to-get-around-in major cities. However, I realize that, by skirting London, I missed a great deal of what the country has to offer. So the city is on my \\"must-see\\" list for my next vacation to England, whenever that might be. In the meantime, I'll travel there from my armchair using Lonely Planet's outstanding \\"London\\" city guide.<br /><br />Some of the recent Lonely Planet guidebooks I've gotten through the Amazon Vine program have been unimpressive, and have even inspired me to offer editorial comments about how good they used to be compared with how they are now. But \\"London\\" is an exception. This is a really good guidebook. Although I lack the first-hand experience with the city to say how accurate or comprehensive it is, I can say for sure that it is attractive, colorful, extremely well organized and exceptionally easy to use. It has a lot of features that I really like. For example:<br /><br />-- All the maps are found together in the last 45 pages--that's in addition to the larger pull-out city map bound inside the back cover.<br /><br />-- The city's vast area is organized into 10 neighborhoods, each of which gets from 15 to more than 60 color-coded pages of detailed information on attractions, restaurants, lodging, cultural activities, shopping, entertainment, drinking and nightlife, and so on.<br /><br />-- Many \\"Top Sights\\" sidebars are scattered throughout, set off by eye-catching blue headings, in addition to the main text. Each offers a \\"Practicalities\\" listing of such things as the page number of the relevant map, website, physical address, admission fee, hours of operation and Tube stop. These are really handy and easy to use.<br /><br />-- The detailed floor plans of such places as the British Museum, the Tower of London, St. Paul's Cathedral and the Victoria & Albert Museum are pretty cool.<br /><br />-- The balance between text and photos tilts heavily toward text. This is not a \\"picture book.\\" The sparse photos in it are relevant to the text but of secondary importance. B006UG90RM|4.0|The last time David Lee Roth sang on a Van Halen record, Ronald Reagan was president, the summer Olympics were held in Los Angeles, and the space shuttle Discovery took off on its maiden voyage. The year was 1984 and Van Halen's sixth studio album, creatively titled \\"1984,\\" shot up the charts thanks to now classic singles like \\"Jump\\" and \\"Panama.\\"<br /><br />Now, 29 years later, Van Halen is back with three of the four original members. The only remaining holdout is bassist Michael Anthony, who was replaced by Eddie Van Halen's 20-year-old son. Although a classic band reuniting is nothing new these days, Van Halen doesn't try to reinvent themselves to capture a new audience. No, \\"A Different Kind of Truth\\" is an album for the now middle-aged original fans who still love to rock and those of us who feel we may have been born in the wrong decade for the music we love.<br /><br />With a classic lineup in tow, Van Halen set out to make a classic record and they did just that. The band doesn't just take things back to \\"1984,\\" \\"A Different Kind of Truth\\" goes all the way back to their self-titled debut. With some of the tracks originating from early demo tapes, Van Halen filled the gaps with tracks written in the same style.<br /><br />One such recently written track is \\"Stay Frosty,\\" a song reminiscent of \\"Ice Cream Man\\" off of their 1978 debut in both name and instrumentation. Eddie Van Halen breaks out the acoustic guitar for the intro, just as he did on \\"Ice Cream Man,\\" but it isn't long before he's finger tapping his way across the neck of his electric. It's no surprise he can still play, but David Lee Roth's voice sounds as if it hasn't aged a day. His mixture of spoken and sung lyrics mixed with the occasional screams of \\"wooo\\" or \\"look out\\" offers listeners a certain level of nostalgia Sammy Hagar was never able to achieve with the band.<br /><br />For anyone familiar with the Sammy Hagar years of Van Halen, it quickly becomes clear on \\"A Different Kind of Truth\\" that David Lee Roth is much better at bringing out the best in Eddie Van Halen's abilities. He does this by constantly fighting Eddie for the spotlight. Every track seems to be a battle for supremacy, but never in a bad way. Each break in the vocal action gives Eddie Van Halen a new chance to show off, but Diamond Dave's knack for a clever and creative delivery of often humorous lyrics offers plenty of competition.<br /><br />On \\"You and Your Blues,\\" for instance, Eddie Van Halen interrupts a relatively straightforward track with one of the more impressive instrumental moments of the entire record. When the vocals return, David Lee Roth screams out the first line of the chorus, \\"woman, you suffer from a color,\\" with a little more aggression than his previous efforts with the same words. The battle is still won by Eddie, but the album-long war is far from over. In the end, the only true winner is the fans. If you loved Van Halen in the '80s, you will definitely enjoy this record.<br /><br />Track Suggestion: \\"Stay Frosty\\"that David Lee Roth is much better at bringing out the best in Eddie Van Halen's abilities. B0041B785Y|3.0|I guess I'll have to apologize in advance for not thinking that every album by my favorite Metal band of the last twelve years is perfect.  I was relatively late to the Meshuggah party, as Chaosphere was when I jumped on the train, and, to date, is still my favorite release (by the smallest of margins).  While DEI is an unbelievable record which possesses one of Metal's greatest tracks, Future Breed Machine, Chaosphere, in my opinion, was simply more consistent from start to finish.<br /><br />To give an idea of where my review is coming from, I should state that Nothing, while totally original in terms of direction, is my least favorite record from these guys.  The first twelve minutes of \\"I\\" is near-perfection.  While Catch 33 is a bold record that shows their songwriting ambition (the ethereal guitar sections are its best feature), it contains too much of Nothing's style of one-note riffing for my taste.  Obzen, in my opinion, was a fantastic amalgam of their post-DEI style.  I'm a huge fan of old-school Thrash, and Combustion was *exactly* the track I was waiting for Meshuggah to make; it's literally Thrash-done-Meshuggah-style.  In fact, I think Combustion ranks right up there with Future Breed Machine.  While the one-note riffs are still there, they incorporated more of Chaosphere's staccato muting, and, for me, the direction was perfect.<br /><br />That brings me to Koloss.  After about a dozen full listens, I think I have developed a sufficient enough opinion.  My expectations were *not* for the record to sound like another record; I just wanted to love it for whatever it is.  Unfortunately, I just *like* it.  It's pointless to mention production value, as Meshuggah know what it takes to make their records sound great, and it sounds as good as ever, here.<br /><br />\\"I Am Colossus\\" is a solid opener, and contains very straight-forward riffing (relative to Meshuggah).  I'm a sucker for triplet-based tracks with this feel, and this one doesn't disappoint.  Tomas was correct when he said that the two notes Frederik plays during the solo are all that was necessary to make it sound great.  The supporting rhythm riff during the solo is, to me, the standout section of this track.<br /><br />\\"The Demons Name Is Surveillance\\" is one of two fast/more driving tracks on the record, and, while its 6/8-double-bass feel is wonderfully played (does anyone really need to continue emphasizing how tight these guys are?), the notes/riffs just don't grab me.  The higher-note bends that are done during what might be called the second Meshuggah-chorus (which is never truly a chorus ;) just seem a bit gratuitous.  The solo isn't particularly noteworthy (no pun intended), as it's Thordendal's typical tapping, and it doesn't sound very inspired.  It sounds as if this track was trying to be the Koloss version of Bleed.  The endings are similar, but the exclamation point put on the end of Bleed is not even close to being matched, here.<br /><br />\\"Do Not Look Down\\" is what I believe a Nothing track *should* have sounded more like.  As a drummer, I love the fact that Haake's pocket is so deep.  Meshuggah isn't what I would call a \\"groove\\" band per se, but the fact that they have such a great pocket makes their brand of Metal that much more enjoyable.  The riffs themselves during this track just have a much freer/less-intentional feel to them, and the solo is one of the best that Thordendal's done in a while.  I love the effect that was added to the last few notes of it!  He took a break from the tapping and let all of us know, once again, that he can own with the traditional picking style.<br /><br />\\"Behind the Sun\\" doesn't grab me, at all.  While Meshuggah are quite capable of producing slow/brooding tunes that are effective, I don't feel that this is one of them.  It's usually a difficult task to make this type tune hold interest, and this one just doesn't quite do it.  I find myself listening *only* because it's Meshuggah playing it, and simply hearing well-played music is sometimes enough for me.  The second half of the tune is serviceable, but that's about it.<br /><br />\\"The Hurt That Finds You First\\", the first two-thirds of which is quite Thrashy, just didn't quite hit the mark until, strangely for me, the speed was over.  I quite enjoy the ambient/relaxed material these guys do, and the outro is nicely done.  Like TDNIS, I just didn't like the notes that they chose to go with the respective feels, and I really do believe that they could have done better.  Take a listen to \\"Terminal Illusions\\" or \\"Combustion\\", and you'll hear Thrash riffs that mean far more than anything played, here.  To me, this tune would be a really good Thrash tune for any other band, but, for Meshuggah, it's merely decent.<br /><br />\\"Marrow\\" is nothing more than a b-side/cutting-floor track from Nothing.  While my opinion may seem slanted because of the song's Nothing influence, I really don't think it would have even stood out if it were *on* Nothing. The only reason to listen the tune is because of Haake's excellent performance.<br /><br />\\"Break Those Bones Whose Sinews Gave It Motion\\" is one of two tunes that's grown on me since I starting listening to it.  This is slowness-done-right.  In context, the riffs really do work well with each other, and the parts never seem gratuitous.  This is a standout track on the record.<br /><br />\\"Swarm\\" is a track that, while solid and consistent with a good feel that they haven't done in years, just never quite takes off as anything special.  There are no riffs that really stand out.  This is yet another missed opportunity, and the ending is just plain lazy.<br /><br />\\"Demiurge\\" is the other tune that has started to grow on me.  In terms of feel, Meshuggah really hasn't ever done a track like this one, and that's why I really didn't know what to think, at first.  It's hard to describe the feel of what might be called Meshuggah-verses here, but it's almost a....rebellious feel?  I know that there's a better word for it, but I'm currently at a loss.  It sort of reminds me of the approach of \\"Up Lift\\" from Reinventing the Steel.  That said, while the majority of the tune is quite good, this is another case where I believe the outro should have been a little less lazy.<br /><br />\\"The Last Vigil\\" is a nice outro that fits quite well with the scheme of the record.  As has been pointed out, it has that Catch 33 feel to it, and the fact that Koloss ends this way isn't at all a detriment.<br /><br />My Standout Tracks:<br />I Am Colossus<br />Do Not Look Down<br />Break Those Bones Whose Sinews Gave it Motion<br />Demiurge<br /><br />I'm sorry for the levity, and I apologize if none of this was helpful/interesting.  I started typing and couldn't stop!  Meshuggah is one of the only bands that brings it out!<br /><br />Meshuggah have the luxury in that they have to *try* to make a bad album.  That hasn't happened here, but it could have been so much more.

0763655465|5.0|In First Things First, bestselling author Steven Covey devotes an entire chapter to the important subject of \\"Integrity in the Moment of Choice\\".<br /><br />In it he writes, \\"A moment of choice is a moment of truth. It's the testing point of our character and competence\\". After listing some of the factors affecting us in the moment of choice he goes on, \\"With all these factors acting on us, it's important to remember that a moment of choice is just that - a moment of choice. Whether we react automatically to one or more of these influences, empower circumstances or other people to control us, or use our human endowments to make a conscious, conscience-directed decision - it's our choice\\".<br /><br />First Things First is a book for adults.<br /><br />Oh No, George makes some of the same points in a presentation that's fun filled and kid friendly.<br /><br />George is some form of generic hound dog. He has a huge snout, long, droopy ears, and large expressive eyes. On the frontispiece he is pictured in that most pooch like of poses; asleep, on his back, belly exposed, paws lifted into the air. Then the action gets underway...<br /><br />Harry, George's owner, is leaving the house. As he leaves he exhorts George to good behavior in his absence.<br /><br />Closing his eyes in innocence, George answers, \\"I'll be very good\\". Everything about his appearance underscores that this indeed is his intention.<br /><br />Absent his master's stabilizing presence however, George is presented with a series of tough temptations (eating cake, chasing the cat, digging up the garden).  Each time the narrator asks the question, \\"What will George do?\\" And each time our canine hero is pictured struggling with the choice of immediate gratification or honoring his promise to behave. The difficulty of this moment of consciousness is conveyed by the imagery: ears outstretched, eyes wide open looking like he is about to be hit by a truck.<br /><br />And each time he falls we hear the words \\"Oh no, George!\\"<br /><br />Harry returns, George is filled with remorse, and the rest of the story is one of forgiveness, redemption, and restoration.<br /><br />While lots of fun to read, Oh No, George makes an evocative point as to an essential life skill that's too often learned too late.<br /><br />It's a really excellent book.no, George!\\"<br /><br />Harry returns, George is filled with remorse, and the rest of the story is one of forgiveness, redemption, and restoration.<br /><br />While lots of fun to read, Oh No, George makes an evocative point as to an essential life skill that's too often learned too late.<br /><br />It's a really excellent book.

B002MQYOFW|4.0|I heard about his trilogy for a while before I finally decided I would go ahead and order the first book to see how I liked it. I am a college student with little free time but I wanted to read this trilogy after all the recommendations. There is no denying that the way the book is written keeps you going back for more. In less than two days I had blazed through the first book, was driving to the bookstore to grab the second book, and once back at home, was online ordering the third. After I finished the trilogy, I felt so emotionally drained and I knew why. I m going to write my review as if the three books were one book since I had the fortune to read through them successively. Before I start, I have to say that I did thoroughly enjoy these books, there are just things I wish that could be different with it. A lot of issues with the book have already been discussed previously such as the rushing of the story line in the third book, the overuse of \\"drugging Katniss then explaining the missed events\\" trick etc. I wanted to touch upon a couple of thoughts I had in addition to those great reviews.<br /><br />I was pretty intrigued by Katniss' character at first for many reasons, and starting out the book I really really wanted to like her. She seemed immature, complex, intense but deeply caring. I felt like there was a lot of room for character growth. As I read on, I began to dislike her more and more. Throughout the series, her immaturity never seems to wane. Her thoughts sound like that of a child, they are very self serving and arrogant. The reader slowly starts to learn that all her \\"heroic\\" actions throughout the trilogy are about making herself look good, or being able to live with herself. She also definitely has trust issues, no matter how much the lead male character confesses his love to her, puts his life on the life for her or even offers to die in her place, she is still so skeptical about his love for her....REALLY? What more does this poor guy need todo??<br /><br />As for the love triangle in this book between Katniss, Gale and Peeta, it seems forced. I feel like the books could have stood on its own without it. The fact that Katniss and Peeta were thrown into an arena to kill each other was complicated enough. Gale's presence was fine, if the author had kept him as a \\"big brother\\" figure throughout the book which he played perfectly. Gale's character was not developed enough for the readers to really be rooting for him seeing as readers were so engrossed in what Katniss and Peeta were going through together.<br /><br />Aside from the lack of character growth of Katniss, some characters were actually very likable. Prim, an innocent little sister who has a natural talent for healing people like her mother. It is only around Prim that Katniss remotely begins to resemble a real human being with real human feelings. Prim is selfless and very mature for her age, too bad the author had to kill her off for no good purpose. Then there is Rue, a little girl that Katniss meets in the arena who like Prim, is also intelligent,selfless, caring and innocent...guess what she gets killed too. So then there's Finnick who develops as a character beautifully. This apparently shallow, crude man turns out to be caring, deep, troubled and hopelessly in love with a mad girl. Hate to admit it, but at one point, I almost wished the story was told from their point of view because their love story was so beautiful and inspiring...buuuuttt you guessed, the author killed him off with no good purpose or contribution to the story line.<br /><br />Then there is Peeta, what a great character, you cant help but like the guy and feel bad for him at the same time. It is hard not to feel bad for this poor guy who has fallen in love with the heroine with no feelings for others, and barely any redeeming qualities. Peeta's character and strength doesnt waiver from beginning to end and I think that is why he is so likable. He is pure, caring, selfless, humane and an advocate for everybody. He genuinely seems to care for all people. Peeta never becomes afraid or selfish in the face of death and shows an unbelievable amount of maturity and wisdom for being his age. His only weakness as a character? His constant, illogical submissiveness to Katniss.<br /><br />I wish sooooo much that there could be another book, not a sequel to this trilogy but a book that runs concurrently with this trilogy, only narrated by Peeta. I m not sure if this has ever happened or if it would be an unprecedented event but I feel like that is what this trilogy needs. Peeta's emotional stability, complex yet humane character could give readers almost a completely different view on the whole event. If we could follow Peeta through the events that took place, through growing up, entering the games, winning them, going through the quarter quells, being taken hostage by the capitol, being brainwashed, rescued and finally being able to settle down with Katniss, I feel that readers would not only be able to appreciate Katniss and the entire situation more, but it would offer a more complete, less dark story and ending. There needs to be much more that happened and happens for the story to just end like that and hearing Peeta's side of it, would give readers a chance to just feel more complete.

1451648537|5.0|Steve Jobs' mystique was elevated even further after the authorized biography by Walter Isaacson hit the shelves a mere several days after his death-and even more after that by the release of the iPhone 4S (for Steve).    Apple devices have become ubiquitous. When In Manila, the sight of white laptops in cafes, white cords dangling from the ears of grade school students, or grandmothers swiping their fingers across an iPad, barely raises an eyebrow anymore. That is how deeply Apple has become a part of our collective consciousness.    The man who helped give the world the Mac, iTunes, the iPod, the iPhone, the iPad, Pixar and its award-winning animated films, has changed the way we live, and his impact resonates across several industries: music, publication, retail, computing, animation, design, and telecommunications.    Steve Jobs by Walter Isaacson reveals the story behind all these groundbreaking products we've come to know so well. More importantly, it is the story of of a remarkable individual told  from a variety of perspectives, and the most resounding voice, of course, is Steve's. Steve's talks openly about life, death, and everything in between. And the author's voice as heard as well; Isaacson , who interviewed Steve on numerous occasions, up to the point where Steve was undergoing chemotherapy, manages to stay objective, doesn't pander to the recently departed, and this non-fiction account is suspenseful, emotional, incredulous, and even hilarious.    Having topped the bestseller lists and given 5 star ratings on Amazon.com, it is difficult to say who is more fortunate: Isaacson for having Steve Jobs, one of the most reclusive pubic figures, as a subject for a biography, or Jobs, for picking Isaacson to write it. Isaacson turned Steve down when he initially approached him. Isaacson surmised to himself that Steve approached him because he had previously written biographies of two of the greatest minds of all time: of Benjamin Franklin and Albert Einstein,and that Steve was already counting himself among their distinguished company.    If that was the case, it looks like they were both right.    Because we often look to the past to search for clues, several chapters are devoted to his formative years. We see how his adopted parents gave in to him because they realized that this was no ordinary child. His college years were spent on the floors of dorms, because he had decided to drop out of his course, and drop in on classes that interested him. He spent several months traveling around India, living in ashrams. When he returned, he hadn't seemed to have reached nirvana, but he had started to abstain from meat, shaving, and taking showers.    His birthplace of Palo Alto was also very significant. There was a technological renaissance going on at the time. In Palo Alto, being an engineer was the pinnacle of achievement. And the neighborhood Steve grew up in was full of them. There were garage computer clubs, and it was here that the 16-year-old Steve and would meet another Steve (Wozniak) and together they founded Apple. With the help of an Intel engineer, they made and sold their first Apple computer. Its casing was made of wood and it didn't have a screen.    His personal life as an adult was tumultuous as well. He had a daughter whom he refused to acknowledge, despite naming a computer (The Lisa) in her namesake. 14 years after he and Wozniak produced the Apple I, he would be fired from the very company he founded. He started NeXT computers and bought Pixar. In moves that still leave MBA's breathless, Steve would sell Pixar to Disney and NeXT to Apple, and later on, deftly regain control of Apple.    A man already worshipped by multitudes of devotees, his marketing genius, seen through his 1984, \\"Think Different,\\" and \\"Here's to the crazy ones,\\" advertising campaigns, his theatrical product launches at Mac World, and his ruthless pursuit of perfection at any cost have become the stuff of legend. And he walked his talk. His return to Apple saw a Jobs who was still thinking differently, and who envisioned the computer as the centerpiece of human activity, a digital hub. Then came the iPod, iTunes, and the rest is as they say, history.    The book takes the enigma, and shows us the real Steve, warts and all, as they say. For a man who was taken too early, the book is 656 pages short, and it makes one wonder, What if? What else would he have accomplished if he had been given more time? But like he said in his Stanford commencement address, \\"Remembering that you are going to die is the best way I know of avoiding the trap of thinking you have something to lose.
