B005LAJ23A|4.0|The biggest criticisms of season two are: slow pacing, too much soap opera drama (or as another reviewer said, \\"too much talkie-talkie, not enough bitey-bitey\\"), not many zombies (until the second half of the season), Chandler Riggs (Carl) being an atrocious actor (but that's also the writers fault for not writing him more natural dialogue).<br /><br />Season two is a lot different than season one, and I'm not talking about the premise. I'm talking about pacing and cinematography, and there's a reason for this change (but I'll get to that later). But first, let's talk about what season two is. Season two is about the farm. You're stuck there the entire time--that's something best known up front. In season one, there were essentially half a dozen locations--in season two, there's essentially three locations but doubled the number of episodes of season one. So you can sort of see there's a problem with pacing. But, pacing is the BIGGEST (and almost only) FLAW in season two.<br /><br />The reason season two feels so different is because Frank Darabont ( [[ASIN:B000P0J0EW The Shawshank Redemption (Single-Disc Edition)]], [[ASIN:B000P0J0EM The Green Mile (Single Disc Edition)]], [[ASIN:B0010X73ZG The Mist (Two-Disc Collector's Edition)]] ) left the show. He was the main producer of season one, and directed the fantastic hour and a half pilot episode from the first season. Here's the thing: by theory, not a whole lot happened season one, but Frank knows how to do a lot with nothing. I mean, technically Shawshank Repemption and The Green Mile were not action packed films, but Frank knows how to get audience reactions. Glen Mazzara doesn't. Glen Mazzara is an action producer (The Shield) that, for some reason, decided to make a very slow-paced season two (especially the first half--I think we all can agree on that). So, the question is, what use is an action producer if he's not making an action TV show? *Shrug*<br /><br />The second half of the season is when I really started enjoying it. Which is a shame, because I loved every episode of season one--and I only loved the a handful of episodes in season two. But season two is always the most awkward season. It's because it's in between one and three. I mean, let's look at books. How many people like Harry Potter and the Chamber of Secrets the most in the Harry Potter series? I bet there's a small handful. So, I can't completely blame Glen, because there are VERY FEW shows in which season two has been \\"better\\" than season one. Breaking Bad might be one of the exceptions, possibly Dexter.<br /><br />Oh, and other than pacing . . . here's another big flaw: Chandler Riggs. The way Tom Hardy trained for The Dark Knight Rises, that's how the Producers of The Walking Dead needs to treat Chandler Riggs, the boy who plays Carl. I think all the fans of the show feel the same way; a lot of his dialogue is too forced; he tries to talk in a deeper voice than he has (and therefore hinders his acting ability); he doesn't know how to realistically act in front of Rick and Lori. Part of the problem is the writers of the show giving him lines that they should have realized that Chandler couldn't deliver.<br /><br />I think this is a very subtle, almost impossible to prove theory, but it makes sense. Mad Men thrives off from its relationship drama; Breaking Bad thrives from its intense dialogue and emotional cliff hangers; The Killing thrives (well, it does have a second season) from being a mystery: therefore most of the audience, who are primarily women, who watch the show are pleased, because they want to be fooled over and over and over again. The formula is simple:<br /><br />Equation: the killer who they thought was the killer wasn't the killer so there's another killer = The Killing (S2)<br /><br />And The Walking Dead looks to its counterparts and . . . it tries to be like them, instead of being what it should be. Look at it this way, Rubicon (a fantistic, compelling show with rich characters, an innovative plot, and was exactly what it needed to be) got cancelled after one season, and the release date on the DVD/Blu-Ray? Your guess is as good as mine. This is what happens to 'shows that don't comform to the norm: they are assassinated. Maybe it's fear that is holding The Walking Dead back from being what it truly needs to be. In the entire second season of The Walking Dead, there are only two episodes that I thought were pure genius and fit the zombie \\"vibe.\\" Only two out of twelve. So much of the first half of the second season was trying to build soap opera elements instead of zombie folklore/Survival Horror elements. The fall of Rubicon might have been an element in Season 2's inability to take risks. Although, like I said, it's hard to prove.<br /><br />Ultimately, if season two of The Walking Dead contains all the episodes of The Talking Dead (with Chris Hardwick) then it's a must buy. But it's just not as good as season one . . . however, if you're a zombie fan, what else do you have to look forward to (aside from Resident Evil 6)?n innovative plot, and was exactly what it needed to be) got cancelled after one season, and the release date on the DVD/Blu-Ray? 0307593312|3.0|I'm giving this book 3 stars strictly out of my own reverence for Haruki Murakami, easily one of my favorite writers ever.  But this book is flat out an exercise in self-indulgence.  It's not that it lacks a hook - no, it has a great story line.  But it goes NOWHERE with that story.  In 925 pages, there are so many red herrings, vanishing characters, time switches and dropped plot lines, I lost track of what the real story was about. The editors at Knopf did a grave disservice to us as the readers and even to Murakami, who apparently is so untouchable, they relinquished their duties as editors.  Someone needed to tell Mr M when to STOP, when to collect his drifting thoughts, when to tighten the plot.  This book could've been half as long and a far, far better read.  As it is, Murakami seems to be channeling Stieg Larsson, with his intricate and unending forays into the minutiae of making coffee or whatever random thread his character happened onto at that moment. I'm so frustrated by this experience, so disappointed by my expectations of greatness.  Maybe I'll just go back and re-read \\"Hard Boiled Wonderland\\" to rinse the distaste out of my head.

0439023521|3.0|INTRO:<br />As I work at store that sells books, I constantly kept on passing by the book aisle and seeing those simplistic book covers (which I think are really tasteful, by the way), and I had even read the backs of the books and they sounded really interesting. They had a post-apocalyptic / dystopian vibe and I was really into that. Even before the film was rumored, people kept on saying how it was such a great book (but there's a catch that I'll explain further in the \\"TARGET AUDIENCE\\" section of my review). I never bought the books because I read A LOT, and I just happened to be reading the Harry Potter series ( [[ASIN:0545162076 Harry Potter Paperback Box Set (Books 1-7)]] ) and The Dark Tower series ( [[ASIN:0451211243 The Dark Tower Boxed Set (Books 1-4)]] ) back to back (which were both great series by the way); and, more than that, since I am an aspiring writer and was writing a book at the time, I didn't really want to read a present tense, first person book at the same time because I write in past tense, third person for the most part--and that could really screw up writing in an off-hand, subconscious way (and it did when I read the book, but it was like a 24 hour flu). The point being, I kept on making excuse after excuse for not reading The Hunger Games.<br /><br />About a month ago, a co-worker finally convinced me to read it (because I'm one of the only people at my work place that READS books, and I was like, \\"hey, it'll give us something to talk about\\"), so I read the book. And I really think it was beneficial for me as an aspiring writer, but at the same time I can't--in good conscience--give this book anything higher than a 3/5.<br /><br />TARGET AUDIENCE:<br />Okay, this is the thing: I'm a guy. I like DARK, SERIOUS FICTION. Authors I like: Stephen King, Clive Barker, Robert McCammon, Cormac McCarthy, Philip K. Dick, Ray Bradbury, Michael Crichton, Arthur C. Clark, Robert A. Heinlein, George R.R. Martin, and Margaret Atwood. If you know who these authors are and did not like this book, then there's a reason why; and if you have no idea who these authors are and LOVED The Hunger Games, then it's the same reason as the alternative: this book is targeted toward young (12-30) females--I'm certainly not going out on a limb by saying that, nor am I saying there cannot be exceptions (because there are always exceptions). Furthermore, everyone I know that has loved The Hunger Games (a handful of people), also loved Twilight; and there's a few reasons for this: Both books are written in the first person (which girls really \\"dig,\\" I guess) and they're both about one girl in a romantic triangle. Girls LOVE the concept of two guys fighting for their attention, but I gotta say that this wasn't fully realized in The Hunger Games--it's more or less expanded upon in Catching Fire and Mocking Jay, but it's definitely there in shadows . . .<br /><br />So, I highly recommend this book for females who love the Twilight series. But I only partially recommend the book for females who didn't like Twilight (because it's too much of the same but with a different premise). And I literally CANNOT recommend this book for a male who has grown up in the age of videogames, because it's just NOT on par with other books out there that are, in my opinion, better.<br /><br />CHARACTERS: 4/5<br />I like the characters. I didn't love them, but I liked them. They did their part. I cannot go into the lengths of dissecting them in the form of a review, because sadly they're too one-dimensional for such an in-depth analysis. But I did like them.<br /><br />WRITING STYLE: 2/5<br />First person narrative; present tense; short paragraphs; simplistic language. In other words, Suzanne Colins didn't take that many risks with style, which is okay. But, since I read a book every week, Suzanne's bland writing style stuck out like a sore thumb and I didn't like it.<br /><br />But, first person, present tense is becoming popular in the world of teen fiction. It's just something that I don't like because it limits the action (since it's first person), and it's just AWKWARD (since it's present tense); it's an unnatural type of voice. Then again, this is personal preference. I prefer third person, past tense (like Harry Potter)--and maybe present tense from time to time. I guess it's because I'm old school.<br /><br />PACING: 2/5<br />As an aspiring writer I analyze the heck out of other peers' writings. The number one thing I observe is that they have a good idea so they let TOO MUCH HAPPEN WAY TOO SOON. Suzanne Colins did the same thing. The first two chapters you were in Katniss's home; the following two or three chapters you were in the Capitol; then the rest of the book you were in The Hunger Games. What that means is that the first two chapters were really fast and then, gradually, it began to lag. That's weird to think about, right? You would think that the Hunger Games would be the most exciting, fast-paced section of the book. Exciting, yes; but not fast-paced--in fact, it was the most boring part of the book because the shift in pacing.<br /><br />CONCEPT: 3/5<br />It's a concept that hundreds of creative thinkers/writers will come up with without realizing it's alread been done before, so I don't honestly believe Suzanne Colins was copying Battle Royale. I really don't. But I do think she should have been a little bit more \\"thorough\\" in her research before writing a book that was essentially a copied-and-pasted premise of another book/movie. Oh well.<br /><br />It is a fantistically dark idea though. Kids killing kids. However, Suzanne Colins never fully reaches the peak of this concept in her story because she was limited by first person: and that's fact. I believe it would have been better multi-perspective and third person, because that way you wouldn't know which characters were going to live and which ones were going to die. You see, I NEVER worried for Katniss because I knew she was going to live . . . I mean, I'm inside her head, right?<br /><br />CONCLUSION:<br />PROS:<br />lively characters<br />short book<br />it'll give you something to talk about with friends<br /><br />CONS:<br />writing style is bland<br />pacing inconsistency<br />falls short of its potential of being truly dark<br /><br />RECOMMENDED READING:<br />[[ASIN:0451196716 The Long Walk]]<br />[[ASIN:1421527723 Battle Royale: The Novel]]<br />[[ASIN:B006L4MX4A Battle Royale: The Complete Collection [Blu-ray]]]<br />  - well, it's not a book, but you get the point.<br />[[ASIN:0451211243 The Dark Tower Boxed Set (Books 1-4)]]<br />  - if you liked the post-apocalyptic / dystopian themes, you'll like the first four Dark Tower books.<br />[[ASIN:0545162076 Harry Potter Paperback Box Set (Books 1-7)]]<br />  - if you like The Hunger Games, you'll love Harry Potter.<br />[[ASIN:0062094106 Abarat]]<br />  - if you're looking for young adult fiction that's actually genuinely dark, I recommend this.<br />[[ASIN:B0073SQWWC lord of the flies]]<br />  - it is the original \\"kids killing kids\\" premise in a semi-post apocalyptic world.n. 0307352145|3.0|Contrary to what the subtitle implies, there is little in the book showing how introversion can be a strength.  There are relatively few mentions of introverts in the sciences and the arts, where introversion is (arguably) an advantage; the author is much more interested in political figures and persons in the business world.  One gets the distinct impression -- through the many admiring comments on dynamic public speakers, and the numerous, and eventually tiresome, anecdotes about public speaking -- that the author is holding up for us as the height of success and accomplishment a person who can speak unafraid and entertainingly in front of a large group.  Possibly the author took a series of pep talks to  management types who lack confidence and turned those speeches into a book, which is fine, if you are an introverted corporate manager in need of a pep talk and that is all you expect out of the book.  However, anyone hoping for a discussion of introversion that doesn't treat it as a handicap, and that addresses in detail areas of life other than public speaking, is likely to share my disappointment.  The one chapter toward the end of the book on spouses of opposite \\"verts\\" was a breath of fresh air after the stultifying atmosphere of the corporate conference room that the previous chapters seemed to inhabit.<br /><br />The book is also flawed in not defining its subject better.  The author is not a doctor, so there is not a rigorous discussion of what exactly it means to be introverted.  The author cherrypicks facts about scientific studies and theories here and there to support her points, without always differentiating introversion from shyness or sensitivity (which the experts to whom she spoke apparently do).  The concept of introversion in the book is fuzzy enough that at times it seems an introvert can be whoever the author wants to be an introvert if she can use that person as an example.<br /><br />Despite that concern, on balance, I actually liked the discussions of the science:  brain chemistry and psychological studies and the like.  Those discussions tend, though, to be scattered among numerous interviews, which seem to be there only to lengthen the book.  For the most part the interviews are meandering, lacking insight, and illustrate nothing in particular about either introversion or the subject of the interview.  What I got out of most of the interviews was the author's fascination with each interviewee's physical appearance, dress, hairstyle, and jewelry (maybe she feels the need to reassure us that the dreaded introvert can at least *appear* to be a normal human being).<br /><br />The author's over-reliance on anecdotes made me skeptical on points I was inclined to agree with.  First example: the chapter on how Asian cultures tend to be more introverted tells a story of Asian-American roommates leaving the dishes to pile up in the sink because they're too introverted to confront one another; this occurs a few pages after the author lauds Chinese culture for its supposed focus on the group not the individual, but the anecdote about the dishes, to me, shows selfishness and irresponsibility, and  left me questioning the earlier observation.  Second example: Steve Wozniak, tech genius and co-founder of Apple, is used as an example of an introvert made good, which is nice, but she totally ignores the extrovert in the room - the late Steve Jobs, who, no offense to Wozniak, is even more identified with Apple's achievements, and is considered even more of a visionary and genius than Wozniak.  So is the lesson that you can be successful as an introvert, but an extrovert in the same environment will be even more successful?<br /><br />One last point:  as a conservative I was a bit irritated that her examples of successful introverts were from the left side of the political spectrum (for example, Al Gore, invoked as an example of someone who is \\"sensitive,\\" merely because of how early he started peddling global warming alarms).  Your mileage may vary on this point, of course, even if you're a conservative, but, if you tend toward the conservative or Republican in viewpoint, be warned that, much as Willie Nelson's heroes have always been cowboys, the author's heroes all seem to be liberals.

0553812173|4.0|Steven Erikson's sprawling series begins, very much in medias res, with this book. Therein lies the problem. You are thrust into the thick of things, surrounded by characters, places, events, and ideas utterly unknown to the first time reader. Problems daunting in any book, but particularly in one with the scope of the Malazan series. Looking back on when I first read the book, I recall being confused, and rereading certain segments a second time (something I normally never do) in order to fully understand them. The positive counterpoint to this is that I'm not the most patient of people. Many times when reading something slow that did not interest me, I have given up on it and quit reading it. This did not happen with Gardens of the Moon, because despite the initial trouble it gave me, I found myself deeply interested by the plot, setting, and characters. Less than a quarter into the book, those problems start to disappear, and the story really picks up. While Gardens of the Moon isn't on the level of Erikson's best in the series, it certainly holds its own, and shows just why he is such a popular writer among fans of this genre.<br /><br />Without giving much away to those that are new to the series, or simply contemplating picking it up, I will say this. The Malazan books, and this one in particular, are largely about various members of the army of the Malazan Empire. A great deal of characters are employed in this book, so if you are a fan of tightly knitted character drama, or large sprawling epics will certainly play a role in your opinion of this book. However, despite the large number of characters and locations featured in the book, Erikson does a very good job of making each distinctive and notable, so you easily find yourself rooting for some and against others, something that I always consider to be a mark of a good writer.<br /><br />If you are a fan of the fantasy genre, and unafraid of tackling a large series with a complex plot, dozens upon dozens of characters, and action going on across various continents, I highly recommend this book and series. If not, I strongly recommend that you give the genre a try... But perhaps with a different book, as the first hundred pages can be a little unforgiving.characters, and action going on across various continents, I highly recommend this book and series. B002MQYOFW|3.0|This seems like a good time to review The Hunger Games, what with the movie destroying the box office and all. This is a dystopian science fiction story about an annual competition in which children fight to the death. They are forced to participate in this as a penance for a civil war that occurred many years earlier. The main character is one plucky teen (Katniss) who volunteers for the ordeal so that her younger sister doesn't have to participate. There is a also a romantic subplot with a slight amount of intrigue in that she has two boys that admire her. I'm sure you can guess what happens to her in the end (hint: there are two sequels).<br /><br />In terms of plot, although it doesn't really have anything groundbreaking (the plot borrows heavily from The Long Walk and Battle Royale), but it's well paced and exciting to read through. I couldn't put it down and stayed up fairly late finishing it. However, it doesn't offer much apart from that. The setting and premise is completely illogical (for enjoyment it's best not to think too much about it): why would the ruling government think that slaughtering children is a good way to maintain peace? It's also implied that the ruling government maintains its wealth and happiness at the expense of the poorer outlying districts, but as described the districts have vastly less population, and given its advanced economy it seems unlikely that the government would really need the labor regardless. The best that can be said about the writing is that it's workmanlike--it doesn't get in the way, but you won't really every get much joy from some particular diction or turn of phrase. It's also unfortunate that the novel focuses so much on the violence of the hunger games themselves, rather than on the characters' feelings about it. Only Katniss shows any kind of nontrivial thought, and yet the potentially interesting love triangle among her and her suitors is barely explored.<br /><br />Overall, if come in not expecting much morethan cheap thrills, you won't be disappointed. After I finished I felt sort of hungover, like I had just consumed some drug that, while giving me an enjoyable high, left me empty inside of any authentic stimulation. But I don't want to understate how fun the book is to read while you are doing it. After all, it's not possible to read intelligent literature all the time, and if you read The Hunger Games instead of watching reality TV, then that's at least some improvement.
